# UWorld-Anki Local Source Map

Explicit UWorld QIDs were not visible in the available sources. This file therefore uses searchable local source references: source file, local question number, and a short question/passage search anchor.

## AminoAcids - Classifications.txt

- Amino acids that can act as nucleophiles... 1. Serine (Ser/S) 2. Threonine (Thr/T) 3. Tyrosine (Tyr/Y) 4. Cysteine (Cys/C) 5. Lysine (Lys/K) -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 6 / search: "act serine threonine"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 10 / search: "act serine cysteine"

- Histidine has an imidazole ring. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "histidine imidazole ring"
- Basic amino acids are positively charged. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 46 of 59 / search: "arginine arg are positively charged whereas those of"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "lysine residues are positively charged and associate with"
- Acidic amino acids are negatively charged. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 46 of 59 / search: "glutamate glu are negatively charged all other amino"
- List of the aromatic amino acids: 1. Tryptophan (Trp/W) 2. Phenylalanine (Phe/F) 3. Tyrosine (Tyr/Y) -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "aromatic trp tyrosine"
- List of the basic amino acids (charged) at physiological pH (7): 1. Arginine (Arg/R) 2. Lysine (Lys/K) -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 46 of 59 / search: "charged arginine arg"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "charged arginine lysine"
- The quaternary structure of a protein is primarily stabilized through non-covalent interactions & disulfide bonds. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "stabilized non covalent"
- In isoelectric focusing, the amino acids that make the peptide's pI high are arginine (R), lysine (K), & histidine (H). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 46 of 59 / search: "peptides arginine lysine"
- Proteins exposed to low amounts of salt become more stable due to a greater number of ionic interactions. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "salt become ionic"
- Anti-parallel β-sheets are linked together by short sequences called β-turns. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 12 of 15 / search: "amino acids linked together by peptide bonds some"
- The 3 amino acids capable of being phosphorylated are serine (S), threonine (T), and tyrosine (Y). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 6 / search: "serine threonine tyrosine"
- Classification of charged vs. uncharged amino acids: - Charged amino acids: includes only acidic or basic (ionized) amino acids. - Uncharged amino acids: includes both polar & non-polar amino acids (non-ionizable). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 59 / search: "charged non ionizable"
- Under certain conditions that allow chiral amino acids to tautomerize, enols are formed as intermediates - resulting in a racemic mixture when they revert back to the ketone form. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 44 of 59 / search: "chiral mixture ketone"
- Parallel β-sheets are linked together by long sequences called 360° loops. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 12 of 15 / search: "amino acids linked together by peptide bonds some"
- The reduction of 1 disulfide bond(s) requires the addition of 2 electron(s), since both sulfur atoms need to be reduced (S-S → SH & SH). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 52 of 59 / search: "reduction electron sulfur"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "reduction electron reduced"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 5 / search: "reduction sulfur reduced"
- "When a disulfide bond is formed (SH & SH → S-S), 2 electron(s) are lost, since both SHs need to get oxidized." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "electron lost oxidized"
- "Mechanism for the hydrolysis of an N-terminal peptide bond: 0. Under acid catalysis, the carbonyl oxygen is protonated first 1. H2O attacks the carbonyl carbon. 2. Carbonyl ""opens"" - forming an sp3-hybridized intermediate. 3. Proton transfers convert the... -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "hydrolysis protonated h2o"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "carbonyl forming proton"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "mechanism attacks forming"

## Biochemistry - Bioenergetics.txt

- Proteins with bonds that require inputs of energy (like ATP hydrolysis) to form are thermodynamically unstable. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "require atp thermodynamically"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "atp thermodynamically unstable"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "atp thermodynamically unstable"
- Proteins with bonds that do not require inputs of energy (like ATP hydrolysis) to form are thermodynamically stable. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "require atp thermodynamically"

## Biochemistry - Carbohydrates.txt

- In Benedict's test, fructose gives a false positive since it can tautomerize to give an aldehyde in its linear form - making it appear as a reducing sugar. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "fructose tautomerize reducing"
- In a sugar's cyclic form, reducing sugars can be identified by the presence of a(n) free OH on its anomeric carbon. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "sugars reducing presence"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 15 / search: "reducing sugars can"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "sugars reducing free"
- Epimers are diastereomers that differ in configuration at only 1 chiral center on carbohydrates. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 15 / search: "epimers configuration chiral"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "epimers differ carbohydrates"
- Are carbohydrates mostly in the linear or cyclic form? Cyclic, since the cyclic form is more stable. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrates linear cyclic"
- α-D-glucose & β-D-glucose are epimers & anomers of each other. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "beta epimers anomers"
- When drawing a carbohydrate's fischer projection into a ring, OH's on the fischer projection... Right: point down Left: point up -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "structures represents the fischer projection of a beta"
- When drawing a carbohydrate's fischer projection into a ring, OHs on the fischer projection... Left: point up Right: point down -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "structures represents the fischer projection of a beta"
- Mutarotation is when carbohydrates spontaneously change between α & β anomers. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrates beta anomers"
- Tollen's reagent uses Ag(NH3)2+ to detect reducing sugars. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "tollens nh3 reducing"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "tollens reagent reducing"
- To test for only glucose's presence in a solution of reducing sugars, glucose oxidase is used. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "presence reducing sugars"
- Glycosides from 5 and 6 membered rings are called... 5: furanoside 6: pyranoside -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "of three 6 membered rings and one 5"
- What's the difference between a complex carbohydrate & a simple carbohydrate? - Complex: multiple carbohydrates (disaccharide/polysaccharide) - Simple: one carbohydrate (monosaccharide) -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 45 of 59 / search: "carbohydrate disaccharide monosaccharide"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 15 / search: "carbohydrates disaccharide monosaccharide"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "disaccharide polysaccharide monosaccharide"
- Sucrose is a disaccharide made of α-D-glucose & β-D-fructose in an α-1,2 linkage. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "disaccharide beta linkage"
- Lactose is a disaccharide made of β-D-galactose & β-D-glucose in an β-1,4 linkage. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "lactose disaccharide beta"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 15 / search: "lactose disaccharide galactose"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "disaccharide beta linkage"
- Maltose is a disaccharide made of α-D-glucose residues in an α-1,4 linkage. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 15 / search: "maltose disaccharide linkage"
- Cellulose is a linear homopolysaccharide made of β-D-glucose residues in a β-1,4 linkage. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "cellulose linear beta"
- Humans cannot digest any sugars with β-linkages, apart from lactose. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 15 / search: "humans sugars apart"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "humans beta lactose"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 15 / search: "humans sugars lactose"
- Can humans digest β-1,4 linkages of sugars? No - think of cellulose (except in the case of lactose) -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "humans beta lactose"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 15 / search: "humans sugars lactose"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "beta linkages sugars"
- What's the difference between maltose & amylose? Maltose: disaccharide Amylose: polysaccharide -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "maltose disaccharide polysaccharide"
- The S/R designation of OH or H on Fischer projections should always be inverted. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "fischer projections always"
- On a fischer projection, what does the horizontal line represent? Wedge -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "reversed in a fischer projection d sugars always"
- On a fischer projection, what does the vertical line represent? Dash -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "reversed in a fischer projection d sugars always"
- D-fructose can cyclize into either a furanose or pyranose. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "cyclized sugars furanose or pyranose because in the"
- Anomers are carbohydrates that differ only by their α or β configurations. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "conformation epimers are carbohydrates that differ at only"
- "Glycogen phosphorylase only cleaves at the non-reducing ends of glycogen that are at least 4 monosaccharides away from a branch point." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "non away branch"
- A carbohydrate's anomeric carbon in its cyclic form was originally its carbonyl carbon in its straight-chain form. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrates anomeric cyclic"
- These sugars are held together by α-glycosidic linkages. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "sugars glycosidic linkages"
- These sugars are held together by β-glycosidic linkages. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "sugars beta glycosidic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "disaccharides linked by beta glycosidic bonds the anomeric"
- When the highest number stereocenter (C5) on a cyclic carbohydrate is R, the linear carbohydrate is D. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "stereocenter cyclic carbohydrate"
- When the highest number stereocenter (C5) on a cyclic carbohydrate is S, the linear carbohydrate is L. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "stereocenter cyclic carbohydrate"
- In a Tollen's test, a silver precipitate indicates the presence of a reducing sugar in solution. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "tollens silver presence"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "tollens silver reducing"
- On a carbohydrate's ring structure, ketals always have 2 non-hydrogen groups bound to its anomeric carbon. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "ring bound anomeric"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrates always anomeric"
- On a carbohydrate's ring structure, acetals always have 1 non-hydrogen & 1 H group bound to its anomeric carbon. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "ring bound anomeric"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrates always anomeric"
- Tollens test can only detect reducing sugars in solutions of acteals, since the linear form of a ketal is instead already fully oxidized. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "tollens reducing sugars"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "tollens reducing sugars"
- In a sugar's linear form, reducing sugars can be identified by the presence of a(n) aldehyde group on C1. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "sugars reducing presence"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 15 / search: "reducing sugars can"
- In Benedict's or Tollen's test, the functional group that acts as the reducing agent is the aldehyde in the sugar's linear form - getting oxidized into a carboxylic acid in the process. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "tollens reducing agent"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "tollens reducing sugars"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "chemical species acts as the reducing agent in"
- A carbohydrate in its cyclic form has 1 more chiral center than it does in its linear form. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrate cyclic linear"
- A carbohydrate in its linear form has 1 less chiral center than it does in its cyclic form. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrate linear cyclic"
- On carbohydrates, α-anomers & β-anomers do differ in optical activity. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "carbohydrates anomers beta"

## Biochemistry - DNA Biotechnology.txt

- How does the chain-terminating method of DNA sequencing work? DNA strands separated by heating → ddNTP incorporated into the growing chain → growth is terminated (due to ddNTP) → differently sized fragments separated via gel electrophoresis -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "terminating sequencing ddntp"
- What's the difference between PCR and DNA replication? PCR... (x2) 1. Uses heat to separate the strands 2. Does not use DNA ligase/helicase/gyrase -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "pcr replication uses"
- In PCR, 92°C separates the strands & 62°C allows the primers to anneal. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "pcr primers anneal"
- Guanine has 2 hydrogen bond donors & cytosine has 2 hydrogen bond acceptors. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "guanine hydrogen cytosine"
- Why are nitrogenous bases exceptionally stable? They're heterocycles containing nitrogen, which delocalizes the π electron similar to an aromatic ring. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "nitrogen aromatic ring"
- DNA's double helix is stabilized by hydrogen bonding & planar base (π) stacking. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "double helix stabilized"
- Cytosine has 2 hydrogen bond acceptors & Guanine has 2 hydrogen bond donors. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "cytosine hydrogen guanine"
- DNA helicase separates the double helix by breaking hydrogen bonds. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "double helix hydrogen"
- DNA polymerase travels along the parent strand in the 3' → 5' direction but synthesizes its transcript in the 5' → 3' direction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "polymerase strand transcript"
- In DNA replication, the leading strand in DNA replication synthesizes in the direction of DNA helicase. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 30 / search: "replication strand helicase"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 30 / search: "replication strand helicase"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 30 / search: "replication strand helicase"
- After DNA polymerase adds a base to the growing strand, pyrophosphate is released. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "happens when dna polymerase adds a deoxyribonucleotide triphosphate"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "happens when dna polymerase adds a deoxyribonucleotide triphosphate"
- Why do researchers incorporate antibiotic resistance into vector plasmids? Allows them to treat the entire plasmid colony with antibiotics - isolating only the desired vector (only they survive) -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 54 of 59 / search: "vector entire desired"
- Cuts from restriction enzymes can create sticky ends, which reanneal easily. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "restriction sticky ends"
- DNA libraries are generated by randomly fragmenting DNA and cloning the fragments into a vector. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 30 / search: "generated cloning vector"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 30 / search: "generated cloning vector"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 30 / search: "generated cloning vector"
- cDNA libraries are made by reverse-transcribing mRNA back into its original DNA. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "cdna reverse mrna"
- Southern blots uses single-stranded DNA as a probe & detect the target DNA's presence by whether it binds its complement. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "uses stranded target"
- Chain-terminating method of DNA sequencing incorporates ddNTPs into the growing DNA strand to terminate its growth. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "terminating sequencing ddntps"
- What risks might retroviral DNA integration (in gene therapy) pose to the host? Could accidentally activate a proto-oncogene to become cancerous. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 40 of 59 / search: "retroviral integration host"
- Histone proteins are positively charged due to their basic amino acids. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 59 / search: "histone positively charged"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 46 of 59 / search: "arginine arg are positively charged whereas those of"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "lysine residues are positively charged and associate with"

- Thymine dimers cause double-stranded breaks in DNA. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "thymine double stranded"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 12 of 30 / search: "double stranded breaks"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 14 of 30 / search: "double stranded breaks"
- In gel electrophoresis, short DNA strands migrate further in the gel. -> Local sources: 05-UWorld_Physics_05222026_IDNoID_15Q.pdf / Question 2 of 15 / search: "gel electrophoresis migrate"; 05-UWorld_Physics_05222026_IDNoID_15Q.pdf / Question 3 of 15 / search: "gel electrophoresis migrate"; 05-UWorld_Physics_05222026_IDNoID_15Q.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "gel electrophoresis migrate"

- Steps to determining if a ring is aromatic using Hückel's rule: 1. Plug the number of π-electrons (2 per π-bond) into 4n+2 = π-electrons. 2. Solve for n. 3. If n is a whole number, the compound is aromatic. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "ring aromatic solve"
- The nitrogenous bases (adenine, thymine, guanine, cytosine) are aromatic. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "the five nitrogenous bases adenine cytosine guanine thymine"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "the five nitrogenous bases adenine cytosine guanine thymine"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "adenine thymine guanine"

- In Sanger Sequencing, ddNTPs are incorporated into the growing strand to terminate its growth, due to the fact that it lacks a C3' OH - preventing the formation of the phosphodiester bond. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "sanger sequencing ddntps"

- Adipocytes release glycerol & free fatty acids to use for energy in the fasting/post-absorptive state. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "glycerol free fatty"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "glycerol free fatty"

## Biochemistry - RNA Gene Expression.txt

- RNA polymerase catalyzes the transcription of the mRNA transcript from DNA's template strand. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "polymerase mrna transcript"
- In prokaryotes, mRNA is polycistronic - meaning it carries the information for multiple genes to encode multiple proteins. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "mrna genes encode"
- In mRNA, the sequences for stop codons are UAA, UAG, and UGA. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "mrna sequences codons"
- During translation, the mRNA's codon & tRNA's anti-codon base-pair in a(n) antiparallel direction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 25 of 30 / search: "translation mrnas trnas"
- The genetic code is degenerate, meaning more than 1 codon can specify for the same amino acid. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "code degenerate codon"
- Mutations that take place on the wobble position of a codon are usually silent mutations, since the amino acid it codes for is often unchanged. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "wobble codon silent"
- Cystic fibrosis results from a frameshift mutation in a codon encoding phenylalanine - resulting in a defective Cl- channel that never exits the endoplasmic reticulum. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 6 / search: "cystic fibrosis mutation"
- RNA polymease attaches to DNA's antisense strand to model the mRNA transcript after the sense strand. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "strand mrna transcript"
- The promoter region's TATA box has a high concentration of adenine & thymine residues, since having a fewer number of hydrogen bonds makes strand separation easier. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "adenine thymine hydrogen"; 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "adenine thymine strand"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "adenine thymine strand"
- RNA polymerase travels along DNA's antisense strand in the 3' → 5' direction to synthesize its transcript in the 5' → 3' direction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "polymerase strand transcript"
- "The TATA box is typically located -25 bases upstream from where RNA polymerase begins synthesizing its mRNA transcript." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "polymerase mrna transcript"
- The 5'-cap added to the hnRNA transcript allows the future mRNA to both survive longer in the cytoplasm & get recognized by ribosomes. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "transcript mrna cytoplasm"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 25 of 30 / search: "added mrna ribosomes"
- The 3'-tail added to the hnRNA transcript serves to limit mRNA's time in the cytosol, since its residues slowly degrade over time. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 25 of 30 / search: "tail added mrnas"
- The mRNA transcript has untranslated regions on both its 3' & 5' ends, since the ribosome only translates from AUG → stop codon. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "mrna transcript ends"
- In the initiation phase of translation, the ribosome's small subunit first attaches to the mRNA transcript's 5' end. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 25 of 30 / search: "translation ribosomes mrna"
- In the ribosome, the P site is where peptidyl transferase (apart of the large subunit) catalyzes the formation of peptide bonds using GTP hydrolysis. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 7 of 10 / search: "site peptide hydrolysis"
- As far as post-translational modifications go, carboxylation of proteins mostly occurs in order to add calcium-binding sites to molecules. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 6 / search: "r domain post translational modifications are covalent additions"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 15 / search: "monosaccharide although post translational modifications can cause gel"
- In a prokaryotic operon, the regulator gene encodes for the repressor/inducer protein that binds to the operator site & regulates transcription. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 6 / search: "regulator site regulates"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 47 of 59 / search: "regulator site transcription"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 48 of 59 / search: "regulator site transcription"
- In repressible operons, repressor proteins cannot function until a corepressor binds - shutting off transcription once they both bind to the promoter. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "repressor transcription promoter"
- The DNA-binding domain on transcription factors binds to specific DNA sequences within the promoter region. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "transcription sequences promoter"
- Response elements are specific DNA sequences located in its promoter region that allow the binding of only very specific transcription factors. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "sequences promoter transcription"
- In order for DNA's enhancer region & promoter region to come into contact with each other, DNA has to bend itself into a hairpin loop. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "enhancer promoter contact"
- Histone acetylases add acetyl groups to lysine residues on histone tails - neutralizing the histone's charge & causing it to unwind from DNA. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 59 / search: "histone lysine residues"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "acetyl lysine residues"
- Histone deacetylases remove acetyl groups from lysine residues on histone tails - restoring the histone's charge & causing it to tighten around DNA. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 59 / search: "histone remove lysine"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "acetyl lysine residues"
- DNA methylase adds methyl groups to adenine & cytosine nucleotides on DNA - silencing gene transcription. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "adds adenine cytosine"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 7 of 30 / search: "adds adenine cytosine"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "adenine cytosine nucleotides"
- The wobble effect is explained by the fact that the 3rd base pair on a codon experiences non-Watson & Crick binding, meaning it can bind even if the last base doesn't match. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "wobble codon watson"
- On a tRNA molecule, 1 anticodon can recognize multiple different codon(s). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "trna anticodon codon"
- Transcription factors are translated in the cytoplasm but wait in the nucleus until they're needed to regulate gene transcription. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 40 of 59 / search: "transcription translated cytoplasm"
- Sense mRNA (5' → 3') is identical in sequence (apart from uracil) to DNA's coding (5' → 3') strand. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "mrna uracil strand"
- Pre-mRNA transcripts are shorter in length than the original gene, due to the lack of regulatory sequences (i.e. promoter region). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "mrna sequences promoter"
- In the lac operon, catabolite activator protein (CAP) stimulates gene transcription by binding to the operator & recruiting RNA polymerase to the promoter site. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "activator transcription promoter"
- On DNA, silencer regions are sequences that contain repressor proteins that prevent RNA polymerase from binding to the promoter sequence. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 32 of 59 / search: "sequences repressor promoter"
- Polyadenylate polymerase catalyzes the addition of adenine residues to pre-mRNA to form the 3' poly-A tail - powered by ATP hydrolysis. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "polymerase mrna poly"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 25 of 30 / search: "mrna poly tail"
- A conservative point mutation in DNA is one where the category (acidic, basic, polar, etc.) of the resulting amino acid is not changed. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "a single point mutation in which the fourth"
- A non-conservative point mutation in DNA is one where the category (acidic, basic, polar, etc.) of the resulting amino acid is changed. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "a single point mutation in which the fourth"
- When miRNAs are somewhat complementary to an mRNA transcript, they silence the transcript's expression by preventing translation. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "complementary mrna transcript"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 25 of 30 / search: "complementary mrna translation"
- When miRNAs are perfectly complementary to an mRNA transcript, they silence the transcript's expression by cleaving it. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 30 / search: "complementary mrna transcript"
- Structural differences between miRNA and siRNA... miRNA: single-stranded hairpin loop. siRNA: double-stranded RNA. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "structural stranded double"
- Structural differences between miRNA and siRNA... siRNA: double-stranded RNA. miRNA: single-stranded hairpin loop. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 31 of 59 / search: "structural double stranded"
- To control for autophoshorylation, researchers should incubate the enzyme with ATP (excluding the substrate) in order to measure the level of phosphorylation associated with just the enzyme itself. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "atp substrate phosphorylation"

## Biology - Musculoskeletal System.txt

- Skeletal & cardiac muslcle cells are striated, while smooth muscle cells are not striated. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "skeletal smooth muscle"
- Smooth muscle is primarily responsible for tone. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "smooth muscle responsible"
- Cardiac & smooth muscles are innervated by the autonomic nervous system. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "spent explanation the autonomic nervous system comprising the"
- The sarcomere is the basic contractile unit of skeletal muscle. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "sarcomere skeletal muscle"
- In muscles, action potentials are initiated on the sarcolemma and distributed through the transverse tubules (T-tubules). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "action transverse tubules"
- Transverse tubules (T-tubules) carry action potentials to the sarcoplasmic reticulum in muscle fibers. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "current close to the sarcoplasmic reticulum sr choice"
- On a muscle cell, the sarcolemma contains the voltage-gated channels necessary for ACh to bind to generate action potentials. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "muscle channels generate"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "muscle necessary action"
- How the action potential spreads in a muscle... Sarcolemma → T-tubules → sarcoplasmic reticulum → Ca2+ released... -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "action muscle tubules"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "action muscle released"
- The sarcoplasmic reticulum releases Ca2+ in a muscle cell. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "close to the sarcoplasmic reticulum sr choice b"
- Ca2+ binds directly to troponin in muscle cells to pull tropomyosin out of the way - allowing for actin-myosin binding. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "ca2 troponin muscle"
- Myosin pulls actin towards the M-line during sarcomere contraction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "myosin actin sarcomere"
- What happens to Ca2+ after the muscle contraction is finished? Ca2+ is taken back into the sarcoplasmic reticulum through active transport. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "pumps c muscle contraction is driven by the"
- Muscle cells exhibit summation in the same way as neurons to generate action potentials. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "muscle generate action"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 30 / search: "neurons action potentials"
- Myosin lets go of actin once ATP binds in a muscle contraction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "myosin actin muscle"
- What are the 4 main tissue types? (x4) 1. Epithelial 2. Muscle 3. Connective 4. Nervous -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "tissue muscle nervous"
- Nicotinic cholinergic receptors are found in skeletal muscle. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "in skeletal muscle"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "t tubules in skeletal muscle cells 5 cytosolic"
- The neuromuscular junction is also known as the motor end plate. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "acetylcholine at the neuromuscular junction to generate a"
- The attachment of myosin to actin in muscle contractions does not require ATP - it only requires Ca2+ to bind to troponin to pull tropomyosin out of the way. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "myosin actin muscle"
- After a muscle contraction, Ca2+ is reuptaken back into the sarcoplasmic reticulum through a calcium-pump that is powered by ATP hydrolysis. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "close to the sarcoplasmic reticulum sr choice b"
- In a muscle contraction, Ca2+ release from the sarcoplasmic reticulum does not require ATP. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "close to the sarcoplasmic reticulum sr choice b"
- In smooth muscle contractions, calmodulin (CaM) first binds to Ca2+ in order to phosphorylate myosin-light chain kinase (MLCK) - which then phosphorylates myosin to allow it to bind to actin. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "smooth muscle ca2"
- Calcium (Ca2+) is involved in muscle contractions for skeletal, smooth, & cardiac muscle. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "ca2 muscle skeletal"
- Sarcomeres are found in skeletal muscle & cardiac muscle - not in smooth muscle. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "t tubules in skeletal muscle cells 5 cytosolic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "in skeletal muscle"
- In smooth muscle cells, thin filaments are anchored to dense bodies - analogous to Z-discs in skeletal muscle fibers. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "t tubules in skeletal muscle cells 5 cytosolic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "in skeletal muscle"
- Can different muscle fibers within the same muscle produce different contractile strengths? Yes, muscle fibers in the same muscle might have different strengths! Each muscle fiber might have a different concentration of actin/myosin. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "muscle actin myosin"
- Different muscle fibers in the same motor unit can produce different contractile strengths, since each might differ in their concentrations of actin or myosin. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "muscle actin myosin"
- In skeletal muscle, Ca2+ channels in the plasma membrane are mechanically-gated channels, while Ca2+ channels in the sarcoplasmic reticulum are voltage-gated channels. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "opening of ca2 channels in the sr membrane"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 40 of 59 / search: "channels plasma gated"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 41 of 59 / search: "channels plasma gated"
- Smooth muscle cells do not require T-tubules to transmit action potentials to the center of the cell. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "smooth muscle tubules"
- Do skeletal, smooth, and cardiac muscle cells respond to inhibitory signals, excitatory signals, or both? - Skeletal: responds to only excitatory signals. - Smooth: responds to both excitatory & inhibitory signals. - Cardiac: responds to both excitatory & i... -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "skeletal smooth muscle"
- Voltage-gated channels found in skeletal, smooth, and cardiac muscle cells needed for the generation of action potentials: - Skeletal: primarily Na+-gated. - Smooth: primarily Ca2+-gated. - Cardiac: both Na+-gated & Ca2+-gated. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "channels skeletal smooth"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "skeletal muscle needed"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 30 / search: "action potentials primarily"
- In muscle tissue, Na+-gated channels are for the fast-acting generation of A.P.s, while Ca2+-gated channels are for the slow-acting generation of A.P.s. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "muscle channels ca2"
- In skeletal muscle, what initiates the power stroke? The dissociation of ADP & Pi. Do NOT confuse this with ATP hydrolysis. ATP hydrolysis had to have happened prior to the initiation of the power stroke in order to generate the ADP & Pi products. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "t tubules in skeletal muscle cells 5 cytosolic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 26 of 30 / search: "in skeletal muscle"
- Fast-twitch muscle fibers have a greater capacity for Ca2+-pumping back into the sarcoplasmic reticulum, since fast-twitch fibers undergo higher rates of contraction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "close to the sarcoplasmic reticulum sr choice b"

## General Chemistry - Equilibrium.txt

- A dynamic equilibrium is when the rates of forward & backward reactions are equal - leading to no net change. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 6 of 6 / search: "dynamic equilibrium net"
- After reaching dynamic equilibrium, the concentrations of the reactants and products can be unequal. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 59 / search: "equilibrium concentrations reactants"
- In the expression for Keq at dynamic equilibrium, the superscripts are each reactant's stoichiometric coefficient (moles). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 15 of 59 / search: "reactants stoichiometric moles"
- In a reversible reaction, the reaction quotient (Q) measures the ratio of products to reactants before reaching equilibrium (Keq). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 59 / search: "reversible products reactants"
- When the volume of a gaseous container is decreased, the equilibrium shifts towards the side with less moles of gas. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "container moles gas"
- When the volume of a gaseous container is increased, the equilibrium shifts towards the side with more moles of gas. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "container moles gas"
- In the expression for the equilibrium constant (Keq), the concentrations of compounds (\[A\], \[B\], etc.) are expressed in units of molarity (M). -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 3 of 30 / search: "concentrations units molarity"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 3 of 30 / search: "concentrations units molarity"

- Will a change in pressure affect the equilibrium position of the below reaction? Yes. Changes in pressure result in changes in volume. Since there is an unequal number of moles of gas on either side, an increase in volume will favor the side with more moles... -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 59 / search: "affect the equilibrium position"

## General Chemistry - Gases.txt

- Gas pressure is the amount of force per area gas molecules exert on the container walls. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gas area exert"
- In gases, collision force is the force exerted by a gas molecule on another gas molecule with the wall. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gases exerted gas"
- Thermometers work when atmospheric gas molecules collide with the glass and transfer their kinetic energy to gas molecules inside - raising the liquid. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "work gas transfer"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "atmospheric gas inside"
- According to the kinetic theory of gases, the collisions of gas molecules are perfectly elastic, meaning no energy is lost (heat). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gases gas lost"
- Lighter gas molecules have less momentum and exert less force against the container walls. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gas exert container"
- Heavier gas molecules have more momentum and exert more force against the container walls. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gas exert container"
- Gas molecules of different masses exert the same pressure against the container walls. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gas exert container"
- Real gases exert lower pressures on the container walls than ideal gases due to their slight intermolecular forces. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "gases exert container"
- Dalton's law says the total pressure (PT) of all the different gases in a mixture is equal to the sum of the partial pressures of each different gas. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "mixture pressures gas"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 15 of 59 / search: "d if the partial pressures of the reactants"
- High pressures cause gases to act more real, since the gas molecules are forced into close proximity - leading to stronger attractive intermolecular forces. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 5 / search: "close proximity attractive"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "pressures gas stronger"
- In the ideal gas equation, T should always be in Kelvin (K). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "ideal gas kelvin"
- According to Henry's law, lower partial pressures (Pgas) of a gas above a liquid surface means the concentration of the gas is higher in the solution. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "pressures gas liquid"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "pressures gas liquid"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "pressures gas liquid"
- In fluid barometers, the liquid inside the tube rises until the total pressure inside (Pliquid + Pempty air) is equal to the atmospheric pressure (Patm) outside the tube. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "an area atmospheric pressure patm refers to the"
- Ions (cations or anions) are highly unstable in the gas phase. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "highly unstable gas"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "highly unstable gas"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "highly unstable gas"
- In fluid barometers, when the Patm is higher outside the tube than the total pressure (Pliquid + Pempty air) inside the tube, the height of the fluid rises to reach equilibrium. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "patm tube inside"
- In fluid barometers, when the Patm is lower outside the tube than the total pressure (Pliquid + Pempty air) inside the tube, the height of the fluid drops to reach equilibrium. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 10 / search: "patm tube inside"
- When the pressure of a gas is significantly increased & the volume is significantly decreased... - Gases experience attractive intermolecular forces. - Volume of the individual gas molecules becomes more significant. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 5 / search: "attractive intermolecular forces"

## General Chemistry - Periodic Table.txt

- Electron affinity is exothermic (when electrons are gained). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "electron electrons gained"
- Electron affinity is the amount of energy released when atoms gain electrons. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "electron gain electrons"; 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 1 of 30 / search: "electron released electrons"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 1 of 30 / search: "electron released electrons"
- A high ionization energy means it's hard to pull off an electron from an atom. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "means electron atom"
- A low ionization energy means it's easy to pull off an electron from an atom. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 5 / search: "means electron atom"
- "To calculate the effective nuclear charge (Zeff) of an electron in a given shell... Zeff = (#protons) - (#shielding electrons)" -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 59 / search: "electron protons electrons"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 52 of 59 / search: "electron protons electrons"; 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 23 of 30 / search: "electron shell electrons"
- Nitrogen has a higher ionization energy than oxygen because its electron configuration is more stable - requiring more energy to pull off the more tightly bound electron. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "oxygen tightly bound"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 15 / search: "oxygen configuration bound"
- Halogens can have positive oxidation states when they're bound to other negatively charged compounds. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 59 of 59 / search: "bound negatively charged"

- Steps to find the net reaction: 1. Multiply each reactant by whatever number needed to make the electrons equal (5e- & 2e-→ 10e-). 2. Combine the reactants into one large equation. 3. Simplify the coefficients by dividing by a common denominator (if possible). -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 5 of 30 / search: "steps needed coefficients"; 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 6 of 30 / search: "steps reactant reactants"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 5 of 30 / search: "steps needed coefficients"

## General Chemistry - Thermochemistry.txt

- Isothermal processes are when the system's temperature remains constant, so the heat added to (Q) the system cancels out work done by (W) the system. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 10 / search: "heat work done"
- Adiabatic processes are when no heat is exchanged (Q=0) between the system & the surroundings, so changes to the system's internal energy (ΔU) are due to work done to (-W) the system. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "heat internal work"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 10 / search: "heat work done"
- In isothermal processes, the area under the curve of a pressure vs. volume graph represents the work done by (W) the system. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 59 / search: "curve graph represents"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 10 / search: "represents work done"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 18 of 20 / search: "graph work done"
- In adiabatic processes, the area under the curve of a pressure vs. volume graph represents the work done to (-W) the system. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 59 / search: "curve graph represents"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 10 / search: "represents work done"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 18 of 20 / search: "graph work done"
- Isovolumetric processes are when the volume of the gas is constant, meaning the system performs no work (W=0), so the system's internal energy only depends on heat added (Q). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "gas work internal"
- The standard state of a compound is its physical state (solid, liquid, gas) where it's most stable. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "solid liquid gas"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "solid liquid gas"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "solid liquid gas"
- Fusion/melting occurs when molecules in the solid state absorb enough energy to transition to the liquid phase. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "melting solid liquid"
- The transition from a solid → liquid is marked by the substance's melting point. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "solid liquid melting"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "mixture of a solid liquid and gas 86"
- In organic chemistry labs, cold fingers are used to purify products by causing the pure substance to sublimate from solid → gas - leaving behind impurities. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 52 of 59 / search: "chemistry solid impurities"
- In a phase diagram, the point at which the equilibriums between the solid, liquid, and gas phases meet is the triple point. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "solid liquid gas"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "of a solid liquid and gas 86 passage"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "solid liquid gas"
- Enthalpy (ΔH) is equivalent to the heat added (Q) to the system under a constant pressure. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 59 / search: "enthalpy equivalent heat"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "enthalpy heat added"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 59 / search: "enthalpy heat added"
- Equation that measures the heat absorbed or released by a substance: -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "that is absorbed or released by the substance"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 59 / search: "that is absorbed or released by a reaction"
- In the equation for the amount of heat absorbed/released (q) by a system, c represents the substance's specific heat (c). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"
- Specific heat (c) is the amount of heat needed to raise 1 gram of some substance by 1 degree celcius/Kelvin. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "heat raise substance"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat raise substance"
- Heat capacity (C) is the amount of heat needed to raise the temperature of some amount of a substance by 1° celcius/Kelvin. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "the molar heat capacity c is the amount"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat capacity c"
- In adiabatic processes, heat transfer (Q) between the system and surroundings cannot happen - the system can only have work performed on it (-W). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "heat transfer work"
- In isothermal processes, heat transfer (Q) between the system and surroundings can happen - the internal energy (U) is always zero, so the heat added (Q) must cancel out work (W) done by the system. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "heat transfer internal"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 10 / search: "heat work done"; 05-UWorld_Physics_05222026_IDNoID_15Q.pdf / Question 7 of 15 / search: "wave amplitude is always zero in this question"
- Equation that determines heat absorbed/released (q) during the phase change of a substance: -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"

- In the equation for the heat absorbed/released (q) during phase changes, latent heat (L) is the amount of heat needed to change solid → liquid without changing temperature. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "heat solid liquid"
- The enthalpy (ΔH) associated with a reaction in the forward direction has the same magnitude but opposite sign as the reverse reaction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 59 / search: "enthalpy magnitude sign"
- "Entropy (S) represents the heat absorbed/released (q) by a system at a given temperature (K) - how ""spread out"" the energy is." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 59 / search: "heat absorbed released"
- Standard free energy (ΔG°rxn) represents the free energy change of a reaction carried out by compounds in their standard states. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 6 / search: "free represents carried"
- Fusion is the same thing as melting - referring to the transition from solid to liquid. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "melting solid liquid"
- Equation for a non-gaseous substance's heat capacity (C): -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat capacity c"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "the molar heat capacity c is the amount"
- A solid has reached its melting point when the mixture is 50% solid & 50% liquid, where the temperature does not change until the mixture is 100% liquid. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 59 / search: "solid mixture liquid"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "solid melting liquid"
- Vaporizing a liquid requires more energy than the energy needed to melt a solid, because liquids have stronger intermolecular forces (like hydrogen bonding). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "liquid solid stronger"
- During phase changes, when latent heat (L) is added without resulting in a change to temperature, that heat is instead being used to break intermolecular forces. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 59 / search: "heat added instead"
- Which compound will be a solid and which will be a liquid at room temperature? Top: Liquid because it has stronger intramolecular forces. Bottom: Solid because it has stronger intermolecular forces. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 59 / search: "top forces bottom"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 59 / search: "solid liquid stronger"
- The temperature of a substance can be reduced by adding either a cooler object or adding a substance undergoing an endothermic reaction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "moles of a substance undergoing a temperature change"
- The temperature of a substance can be increased by adding either a warmer object or adding a substance undergoing an exothermic reaction. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 1 of 10 / search: "moles of a substance undergoing a temperature change"
- From a phase change diagram, the slope of the line (ΔT/q) is inversely proportional to the phase's heat capacity (C). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 7 of 59 / search: "l and is inversely proportional to the samples"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat capacity c"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 53 of 59 / search: "area and is inversely proportional to the square"
- What's the difference between these two equations? - q = mL → heat gained/released by a substance when it is in the middle of a phase change. - q = mcΔT → heat gained/released by a substance when it's NOT in the middle of a phase change. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat released substance"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 59 / search: "heat gained released"
- What's the difference between these two equations? - q = mcΔT → heat gained/released by a substance when it's NOT in the middle of a phase change. - q = mL → heat gained/released by a substance when it is in the middle of a phase change. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "heat released substance"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 4 of 59 / search: "heat gained released"

## Organic Chemistry - Aldehydes Ketones.txt

- When either aldehydes or ketones are named as substituents, use the prefix oxo-. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 55 of 59 / search: "functional groups are named as substituents which include"
- Aldehydes & ketones have higher boiling points than regular alkanes due to the increased intermolecular attractions incuded by their dipoles. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 5 / search: "intermolecular attractions dipoles"
- "Attacks from nucleophiles on carbonyls without good leaving groups will ""open"" the carbonyl, become reduced to an alcohol, and add the nucleophile." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "attacks nucleophiles leaving"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "attacks good leaving"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "attacks leaving nucleophile"
- "Attacks from nucleophiles on carbonyls with good leaving groups will ""open"" the carbonyl, add the nucleophile, and reform itself back into a carbonyl." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "attacks nucleophiles leaving"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "attacks good leaving"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "attacks leaving nucleophile"
- Acetal formation requires an acidic environment in order for an acid to protonate a hemiacetal's first OH to H2O - causing it to leave & allowing a second alcohol to attack. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "requires h2o attack"
- The formation of acetals/ketals from hemiacetals/hemiketals proceeds as an SN1 nucleophilic substitution. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 57 of 59 / search: "sn1 nucleophilic substitution"
- Imines are formed when NH3 nucleophilically attacks a carbonyl. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 6 / search: "side chain nucleophilically attacks a phosphate group from"
- LiAlH4 acts as a strong reducing agent when treated with an aldehyde, ketone, carboxylic acid, or ester. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "reducing agent ketone"; 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "ketone carboxylic ester"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "ketone carboxylic ester"
- The α-carbon adjacent to a carbonyl is highly acidic due to both the weakness of its C-H bonds and its highly stable conjugate base (enolate). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "adjacent carbonyl conjugate"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 42 of 59 / search: "carbonyl conjugate enolate"
- In keto-enol tautomerization, the equilibrium heavily favors the ketone. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "keto enol ketone"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "keto enol ketone"
- "1,5-diketones undergo aldol condensations to form 6-membered rings." -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "of three 6 membered rings and one 5"
- Diketones undergo intramolecular aldol cyclizations after being treated with a strong base - deprotonating the α-carbon for it to attack the other carbonyl. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "intramolecular aldol attack"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 42 of 59 / search: "intramolecular aldol carbonyl"
- Mechanism of keto-enol tautomerization in acidic conditions: 1. Ketone's oxygen is protonated by H3O+. 2. π-electrons move onto the oxygen to form OH. 3. H2O deprotonates the α-carbon to form the adjacent double bond. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "protonated h3o h2o"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 40 of 59 / search: "mechanism move double"; 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "keto enol acidic"
- Mechanism of keto-enol tautomerization in basic conditions: 1. Ketone's α-carbon is deprotonated by OH-- forming the adjacent double bond 2. π-electrons move onto the oxygen to form O-. 3. O- deprotonates H2O to form OH. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "forming adjacent double"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 40 of 59 / search: "mechanism double move"

If the dicarboxylic acid lost its other proton, the two negatively charged carboxylate groups would cause extreme instability. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "proton negatively charged"

- Tertiary amines cannot react with carboxylic acids to produce amides, since the attacking amine needs to lose a hydrogen to form H2O for the leaving group. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "since needs leaving"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "amines carboxylic amides"
- Esters are named by writing the substituent on O- first and then replacing the -oic acid from the parent carboxylic acid with -oate. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 55 of 59 / search: "named substituent parent"

- "Asymmetrical anhydrides are named by writing both side chains alphabetically and replacing ""acid"" on the parent carboxylic acid with ""anhydride.""" -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 55 of 59 / search: "named chains parent"
- Amides are hydrolyzed into carboxylic acids & ammonia (NH3) when placed in highly acidic or basic solutions. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "hydrolyzed carboxylic acidic"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "hydrolyzed carboxylic acidic"
- Esterification reactions are accelerated in acidic solutions, because it allows the carbonyl's oxygen to get protonated - making the carbonyl carbon more electrophilic. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "acidic carbonyls carbonyl"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "acidic carbonyls carbonyl"
- Aldehydes/ketones are more reactive than amides, carboxylic acids, and esters. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "aldehydes ketones carboxylic"
- The 2 ways esters can be converted into carboxylic acids: 1. Hydrolysis in either acidic or basic conditions. 2. Saponification (NaOH) followed by the addition of a(n) acid. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "hydrolysis saponification naoh"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 2 of 30 / search: "hydrolysis saponification naoh"

Aqueous base-catalyzed hydrolysis (NaOH(aq)), since the hydrolysis of an anhydride results in excess carb... -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 47 of 59 / search: "amide anhydride phase"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 2 of 10 / search: "catalyzed hydrolysis charged"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 7 of 10 / search: "catalyzed hydrolysis charged"

## Organic Chemistry - Common Organic Reactions.txt

- Amines & amides act as bases due to the lone pair on their nitrogen atoms. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "amines amides nitrogen"
- SN1 reactions require the formation of a carbocation before the nucleophilic attack occurs. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "sn1 carbocation nucleophilic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 57 of 59 / search: "sn1 nucleophilic attack"
- SN1 reactions form racemic mixtures since they form carbocation intermediates. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "sn1 since carbocation"
- SN2 reactions are limited both by the nucleophile & leaving group. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "sn2 nucleophile leaving"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 9 of 59 / search: "sn2 nucleophile leaving"
- Rate equation for SN2 reactions... rate = k\[substrate\]\[nucleophile\] -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "sn2 substrate nucleophile"
- Oxidizing & reducing agents typically act first on the functional group with the highest priority. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 55 of 59 / search: "to provide the highest priority functional groups with"
- The α-carbon next to a carbonyl is more acidic due to the resonance stability of the enolate. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 42 of 59 / search: "carbonyl resonance enolate"
- SN1 reactions prefer tertiary 3° carbons, due to the carbocation stability. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "farnesyl impact the carbocation stability directly impact both"
- Aldehydes & ketones can temporarily be turned into acetals or ketals for protection from oxidants or reducing agents. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "aldehydes ketones reducing"
- Treating carbonyls with R-MgBr will reduce the carbonyl → alcohol & add the R-group to the old carbonyl carbon following a(n) acidic workup. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "carbonyls carbonyl acidic"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 24 of 30 / search: "carbonyls carbonyl acidic"
- SN1 & E1 reactions prefer protic solvents since it aids in carbocation stability. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "sn1 since carbocation"
- In the Williamson ether synthesis, the strong base (NaH) deprotonates the alcohol to make it more nucleophilic to attack the alkyl-halide (MeI). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "nucleophilic attack alkyl"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "nucleophilic attack alkyl"
- BH3/THF acts as a reducing agent that prioritizes carboxylic acids first. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "reducing agent carboxylic"
- SN1 reactions can sometimes take place with primary leaving groups when they're on an allylic double bond position. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "sn1 leaving allylic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 57 of 59 / search: "sn1 leaving allylic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "sn1 leaving allylic"
- SN1 reactions can sometimes take place with primary leaving groups when they're on allylic positions due to the resonance stabilization of the carbocation. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "sn1 leaving allylic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "sn1 leaving allylic"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 57 of 59 / search: "sn1 leaving allylic"
- Amines and amides can only be ionized by strong acids. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "reactions 12 amines and amides subject unit chapter"
- Free radicals are compounds with an odd number of valence electrons & a(n) incomplete octet - leading to high reactivity. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 58 of 59 / search: "valence electrons a"
- Zinc (Zn) acts as a(n) reducing agent, since it can easily oxidize itself into Zn2+. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "reducing agent oxidize"
- Copper (Cu) acts as a(n) reducing agent, since it can easily oxidize itself into Cu2+. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "reducing agent oxidize"
- When a carboxylic acid is treated with R-MgBr, MgBr adds itself to the nucleophilic oxygen - forming O-MgBr & an alkane byproduct. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "adds nucleophilic forming"
- Weak nucleophiles result in SN1 substitution reactions. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "nucleophiles sn1 substitution"
- Strong nucleophiles result in SN2 substitution reactions. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 56 of 59 / search: "nucleophiles sn2 substitution"
- In intramolecular esterification reactions, the carbonyl carbon is initially reduced to an alcohol after being attacked, but it eventually reforms the carbonyl in the final cyclic product. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "intramolecular carbonyl final"
- DIBAL is a(n) reducing agent that's capable of converting carboxylic acids/esters into aldehydes. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 5 of 10 / search: "reducing agent carboxylic"
- Michael addition reactions are when enolates nucleophilically attack the β-carbons of π-bonds adjacent to a carbonyl. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "michael attack beta"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 42 of 59 / search: "michael beta carbonyl"
- The Robinson Annulation reaction is a combination of 2 reactions: Michael addition & aldol condensation. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 40 of 59 / search: "40 44 the robinson annulation is a three"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 42 of 59 / search: "40 44 the robinson annulation is a three"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 43 of 59 / search: "40 44 the robinson annulation is a three"
- Alkylation is the process of adding an alkyl group (R-) to nitrogen atoms during amine synthesis. Overalkylation occurs when more than one alkyl group is added to nitrogen, leading to secondary amines (R2NH) or tertiary amines (R3N) instead of the desired p... -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 48 of 59 / search: "alkyl amine secondary"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 10 of 10 / search: "alkyl nitrogen amines"

## Organic Chemistry - Lab Techniques.txt

- In UV spectroscopy, electrons with small energy gaps between their highest-occupied orbital (HOMO) & lowest unoccupied orbital (LUMO) are easier to excite. -> Local sources: 06-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q.pdf / Question 23 of 30 / search: "electrons occupied orbital"; 08-UWorld_Physics_05212026_ID411469881_30Q-Results-Merge.pdf / Question 23 of 30 / search: "electrons occupied orbital"
- NMR spectroscopy uses magnetic fields to orient the nuclear spins of certain protons in the same (α) or opposite (β) direction - which get excited by radiofrequency irradiations. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 38 of 59 / search: "nuclear protons beta"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 36 of 59 / search: "uses magnetic fields"
- In NMR spectroscopy, the x-axis represents chemical shift (δ), with the units parts per million (ppm). -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 30 of 59 / search: "expressed in parts per million ppm which is"
- Thin-layer chromatography uses either silica or alumina as the medium for its stationary phase. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 44 of 59 / search: "chromatography stationary phase"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 10 / search: "monitored by thin layer chromatography tlc when the"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 6 of 10 / search: "monitored by thin layer chromatography tlc when the"
- Paper chromatography uses cellulose as the medium for its stationary phase. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 44 of 59 / search: "chromatography stationary phase"
- In reverse-phase Paper/TLC, the plate is non-polar & the solvent is polar. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 47 of 59 / search: "phase polar solvent"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 49 of 59 / search: "phase polar solvent"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 51 of 59 / search: "phase polar solvent"
- In normal-phase chromatography, the stationary phase is polar & the mobile phase is non-polar. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 44 of 59 / search: "phase chromatography stationary"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "chromatography stationary mobile"
- In reverse-phase chromatography, the stationary phase is non-polar & the mobile phase is polar. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 44 of 59 / search: "phase chromatography stationary"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 3 of 59 / search: "chromatography stationary mobile"
- In affinity chromatography for the separation of proteins, nickel lines the column to bind onto the protein's histidine residues. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 12 of 15 / search: "chromatography column histidine"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 15 / search: "chromatography column histidine"
- In affinity chromatography, proteins are removed from the column by pouring in more receptors that compete with the receptor its bound to. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 44 of 59 / search: "chromatography column receptor"
- In gas chromatography, compounds with lower boiling points will elute first, while higher boiling points will elute last. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 12 of 15 / search: "chromatography lower elute"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 15 / search: "chromatography lower elute"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 8 of 10 / search: "chromatography lower elute"

## Physics - Atomic Nuclei Nuclear Decay.txt

- From the photoelectric effect, photons of light with high intensities generate larger currents (I) from the ejected electrons. -> Local sources: 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 11 of 20 / search: "photons intensities electrons"; 01-All-Uworld-Files.pdf / Question 13 of 20 / search: "photons intensities electrons"
